B006TZBGBW|3.0|Despite the large amount of Metal I listen to, Judas Priest had been largely absent from my collection until only a year or so ago. I blame the mainstream media for doing a terrible job of selling me on them; rather than pushing the strength of the music itself, they pushed Priest's image--ya know, the mid-life crisis motorcycle fetish and black S&M leather, made all the more embarrassing how often it was emulated in light of Halford's homosexuality. Further, you can get a good deal of enjoyment out of Black Sabbath - Paranoid or Iron Maiden - Number of the Beast (i.e. the albums everyone and their mother tells you to check out), whereas British Steel and Screaming for Vengeance are really quite poor representations of Judas Priest's stellar discography. As such, Priest had always felt to me like KISS--that band your dad's generation seemed to idolize but you simply can't take seriously. A few sessions with Sad Wings of Destiny and Sin After Sin resolved that, and JP's enormous talent is now clear to me.<br /><br />As such, it came time to stop listening to downloaded albums and go about legitimizing my collection; I always knew I'd have to do it eventually, but figuring out which editions to buy and whether I would try to grab them all at once or spend years buying the odd album here and there when I had some spare cash is always a daunting experience. The announcement of a huge Judas Priest boxset, one containing every Halford album, retailing for $200 sounded intriguing. Particularly was the fact that the traditionally shunned first two albums would be receiving fresh Remasters for this collection, and that Sad Wings of Destiny would finally get a US release with the proper track order (sides A and B were famously flipped on the US vinyl, greatly damaging the flow of the album). On top of that, every album was to come in a mini-LP replica of the original vinyl editions (having had a few mini-LP's myself, I can vouch that well-done mini-LP's are truly a joy to possess). To boot, Amazon seemed to be selling this boxset at roughly half that price for those who preordered. That was enough to make me bite.<br /><br />Having finally arrived, and having had enough time to sit down with it and listen to a few of the albums, I can't help but feel this had the makings of an awesome collection, marred by cheap packaging and a general feeling of laziness on Sony's behalf.<br /><br />Firstly let me get the good down, lest I feel like I'm whining throughout the entire review. You are getting a huge amount of stuff in this box--17 albums, in fact. In one fell swoop, for a mere $105 (assuming you paid what I paid) you've grabbed every Halford-era Judas Priest album and nearly their entire discography. For the price, you're paying about $6 an album. That's not bad, considering they're new, and at least two of them are remastered. The remasters themselves sound fine, if nothing special. I compared Sad Wings (the only album I listened to all the way through) to a previous pressing of the album I had and didn't notice much of a difference--maybe a slight increase in clarity. If anything, I'm just happy the mastering was NOT compressed to hell and back for the sake of the loudness war. Purists will be glad to know that every album here is presented in its British editions--Rocka Rolla contains the original bottlecap cover (I preferred the Steel Tsar cover, but oh well) and Hell Bent for Leather is restored to its British title, \\"Killing Machine\\" on the spine. As best I can tell nearly all of these albums (i.e. anything that's not the first two or the last two) seem to be identical the Columbia remasters from 2001, containing the same bonus tracks. Given that they were already \\"remastered,\\" I guess it would have been a bit much to expect re-remasters specifically for this box. I think they sound fine, but be aware that you probably already have them.<br /><br />Where this box really falls apart is in its packaging. First, the box is clearly marked \\"The Complete Albums Collection,\\" which is an outright lie. Anyone who's looked at the package contents will immediately realize that the albums from the Ripper Owens years--Jugulator and Demolition--are missing. Aside from the fact that calling something \\"Complete\\" when it's not is deceptive, the omission of these two albums is all the more puzzling, given that economy or size was evidently not a concern when they decided to include no fewer than three live albums in the mix. \\"Unleashed in the East\\" has something of a reputation and its inclusion I can understand (particularly since it was so cleaned up in the studio that it might not even qualify as a true \\"live\\" album), but can anyone honestly tell me that they would rather have \\"Priest...Live!\\" and \\"A Touch of Evil: Live\\" in a box more than a complete studio discography? Halford loyalists be damned. Secondly, this box feels cheap. Really cheap. The box itself is a measly cardboard foldout that feels like it can't possibly be housing seventeen albums. Until you realize, that is, that the albums themselves are similarly housed in paper-thin \\"mini-LP replicas\\". Hard as they try to make those sound like a quality, collectible option, they are anything but--we would have honestly been better served by Digipaks. The cardboard sleeves do not open at all except for the right-hand slit, through which you will, agonizingly, try to pull your poor CD out. Let me be clear here when I say that these are NOT mini-LP replicas. Not the kind that garner such praise from music collectors. The mini-LP's you spend a pretty penny importing from Japan have a great deal of care put into them to ensure that they are exact scale replicas of the original vinyl pressings, from the size of the box to the inserts:  if the original vinyl package contained a poster, the mini-LP's contained an exact scale poster inside as well. As said before, those are a joy to own--these, by contrast, feel like the original vinyls were hastily scanned andcropped onto pieces of cardboard. They contain nothing other than the CD's themselves (which themselves abandon any notion of being true \\"replicas\\" by posting fresh copyright information on the CDs). The \\"booklet\\" included with the box is the bare minimum of what would qualify as an accessory. Within the booklet are a handful of photos, a brief forward, and a compilation of liner notes for each album (again, seemingly lifted directly from the 2001 remasters where applicable). They couldn't even get someone from the actual band to write some kind of retrospective? It's deplorable. There aren't even any lyrics included.<br /><br />What I'm ultimately left with is a collection that doesn't feel at all collectable. I was incredibly excited to buy this, but honestly, after ripping the two remasters and maybe one or two others to compare, this will probably just gather dust on myself. I see no reason to pull it out terribly often--why should I? To stare at the tiny cover art while I listen to the albums? There was very little love put into this product. Nothing you haven't seen before, and in fact if you owned every vinyl or CD pressing before you might find substantially LESS than you already had, considering the lack of lyrics. It makes me wonder who exactly this box was targeted at:  there is literally nothing to attract hardcore Judas Priest fans, given that they'll already have these albums sans the Rocka Rolla and Sad Wings remasters, and newcomers to JP probably aren't going to try to buy their entire discography at once. This box is neither as satisfying as a REAL Collector's Box (like Black Sabbath's excellent Black Box) nor owning each individual album in its own CD case where they can be proudly displayed on one's shelf. It instead feels like the \\"budget box\\" of various DVD collections, where if you don't feel like spending the bucks for lavish editions of some movie collection or TV series, you can instead spend $40 or so to get the entire show on a core set of ten DVDs stacked on top of each other on a spindle inside a barren box.<br /><br />While this will no doubt get much lower scores from Priest loyalists, I've given it something decent, because it served my purposes:  I wanted to \\"clean\\" my collection of Judas Priest downloads as cheaply as possible. And at $6 a pop, this did just that. But that's about all it did. Even the great value starts to come into question when you realize that the average Judas Priest album can be bought for $6-9 on Amazon anyway.<br /><br />I'm disappointed, but for what I paid I really can't complain. If you can get over the fact that there are absolutely no extras here, you're getting a collection of fantastic music for a very reasonable price, this is still a decent buy. Assuming you paid a very reasonable price, that is. The $200 retail seems absolutely ridiculous when I think about it, and really anything more than $130 or so feels like a waste unless you own no JP albums to begin with. Otherwise you're better off just waiting for the Rocka Rolla and Sad Wings remasters to be released individually (you know they will be) and fill in whatever holes you have yourself.

Yes, there are a lot of plot points that seem placed there to elicit wide eyes and wonderment and not necessarily to make sense, but in the scope of the film it really doesn't matter much since the cast sells everything.<br /><br />So, if you are in the mood for an exciting thrill ride with a memorable villain, this is your ticket.  Just, don't expect it to be the smartest thriller you've ever seen.

B007I2BZIE|5.0|I am a Rush fan, but I don't give everything that they ever did 5 stars, although everything that they've ever done is at least above the bar of whatever the mainstream was at the time. They started their careers with a heavily Cream influenced debut, threw in some New Wave into MOVING PICTURES, SIGNALS and GRACE UNDER PRESSURE, some alternative in COUNTERPARTS... well, you get the picture. In every incarnation, they always managed to maintain a certain degree of integrity and virtuosity in their music.<br /><br />I am writing this review after two listens. I listened the second time with headphones while reading the lyrics and I felt the way that I felt when I listened to MOVING PICTURES and 2112 for the first time. I was drawn in and transfixed by the sound and the lyrical content. In this case, this album does not only rock, it soars! The band has been flirting with this sound since 1996's TEST FOR ECHO. As a matter of fact, the chromatic flourishes of Caravan are reminiscent of the ones used in that album's title track. The arpeggio in Wish Them Well is also similar to the one used in Carve Away The Stone, also from TEST FOR ECHO. There are other flourishes throughout that remind me of one song or another. Entre Nous made a cameo in there somewhere and Headlong Flight is an evolved version of something that would've fit well on FLY BY NIGHT. These are the calculations that I allude to with the title to this review. There is enough here that is familiar to draw in long-time fans that complain that their Rush don't sound like they used to, when in fact they've been revisiting their 70's sound on every album beginning with COUNTERPARTS!<br /><br />The allusions aren't so obvious that they are annoying, they merely add familiarity to the sound of this album. Here we have the band running on all 3 cylinders, (pardon the pun) or rather as a V-6! Since TEST FOR ECHO, Neil Peart has been writing from a much more cynical and mature point of view. The song Clockwork Angels is thematically similar to Freewill from PERMANENT WAVES, but where Freewill is written from a brash, in-your-face, rebellious stance, Clockwork Angels is written from an experienced, grown man's perspective. On this album Peart has distilled his ever more piercing views of society, religion and just life in general and married those views to his personal tragedies that he endured after losing his wife and daughter. You hear a lot of pain and anguish throughout the album and it is tempered with resolution and grace. Peart has never written more personally or honestly than on this album. This is by far their best realized concept album and Peart's best writing ever. The album has the same heavy sound and bleak soundscapes that were the centerpiece of the previous album, SNAKES AND ARROWS. There is, however, an added dimension of positivity and grandeur that was severely lacking in that album.<br /><br />After listening 7 times I changed my review from 4 to 5 stars and I placed Clockwork Angels as their third best album of all time. It is definitely the best concept piece they've ever done and the songs are so complex that I completely understand why this would take 5 years to produce. Listen to this and then listen to PERMANENT WAVES OR MOVING PICTURES. CLOCKWORK ANGELS gives them a run for their money. I only place CLOCKWORK ANGELS third place behind MOVING PICTURES and PERMANENT WAVES because those two are still more instantly accessible but CLOCKWORK ANGELS rocks harder and the lyrics are more amazing than anything they've ever done. Geddy's singing has improved and his range has a new fuller quality that I hadn't heard before. Neil is playing more in the background but with every bit of mastery that he can muster. Alex shines throughout. This album has the distinction of having the best ballad ever recorded by the band in The Garden. It is an epic ending to the concept of the album and closes things with a sort of magic that lingers. (Spirit of Radio anyone?) Ithink in time Rush fans will look back and agree that this is one of their top three albums.<br /><br />The story seems to be a metaphor for modern life. I picture the boy of the story as a model of Neil Peart growing up. The Watchmaker is God and the Clockwork Angels are the stars and planets, representing anything that humans have ever worshipped. The Anarchist is Peart himself rebelling at consumerism, blind faith and all the forces that pull us in different directions. As he matures he realizes how many mistakes he makes. The Halo Effect is about how we idealize things; in the case of the story, about how we idealize women. Carnies is about finding a life calling and pursuing it. Again alluding to Peart's own decision to pursue show business? Seven Cities of Gold is about how money rules a large part of our lives and how we are always chasing it. The Wreckers is about the people that start wars and ruin things instead of building things that matter. Headlong Flight is about the long journey that life is. Wish Them Well is about making amends with the people that make other's lives miserable instead of enriching them and their own in the process. The Garden is the brilliant conclusion that our hero, (Peart in disguise?) comes to when he realizes that:<br /><br />The measure of a life is a measure of love and respect<br />So hard to earn, so easily burned<br />In the fullness of time<br />A garden to nurture and protect<br /><br />In other words, success isn't measured by what you achieve in life, but rather by what people think of you while you live your life and what kind of garden you plant. So true,....<br /><br />I would highly recommend this album to anyone that loves art-rock, prog-rock, Rush and cerebral music. This might not be the best album to get to know Rush for the first time since it is a dense concept album and that might offset some people. I easily rank this in their top three albums and there is a lot to digest with repeated listens.<br /><br />My favorite Rush albums follow in order from best to least:<br /><br />MOVING PICTURES<br />PERMANENT WAVES<br />CLOCKWORK ANGELS<br />COUNTERPARTS<br />2112<br /><br />The rest of their albums vary greatly in both style and quality. They all have great moments but can all easily take 6th place. HOLD YOUR FIRE and PRESTO are probably the worst of the bunch and are best saved for last if you want to explore this great band. I would rank the rest of the albums as follows, but this list changes often with my mood:<br /><br />4 stars<br />GRACE UNDER PRESSURE<br />SIGNALS<br />ROLL THE BONES<br />TEST FOR ECHO<br />POWER WINDOWS<br /><br />3 1/2 stars<br />VAPOR TRAILS<br />A FAREWELL TO KINGS<br />HEMISPHERES<br />FLY BY NIGHT<br />CARESS OF STEEL<br />SNAKES AND ARROWS<br /><br />3 stars<br />RUSH<br />HOLD YOUR FIRE<br />PRESTO<br /><br />Happy hunting!

I have to say that this will be up there in my favorite Rush album category.  Not a single t urd on this release.  Great job, Rush!

B0071K5KWU|5.0|Okay, I admit I've been a huge Banks fan for some 35 years. His music with Genesis I always found very moving and easy to relate to. But the side of his music I always loved the most was the beautiful, detailed instrumental side, and that is what we get here. Now I liked 'Seven', but 'Six' is a marked improvement. Every piece is lush and emotional, often complex, but at the same time, often pastoral, recalling Vaughan-Williams, and perhaps even more reminiscient of Delius' Brigg Fair. But most of all it sounds like Tony Banks, his signature chord stylings are present and work beautifully in this orchestral setting where his exotic chords are well utilized, something somewhat missing on 'Seven'. It's hard to pick a favorite piece, but certainly 'The Blade' is stunning-not in a technical sense, but emotionally. If this doesn't bring tears to your eyes, I'm not sure if anything will-it's that good! This has become a very personal cd for me, and, odd as it may sound, is probably the most 'perfect' cd I've ever heard.

1612130283|1.0|I can no longer live in a reality where Fifty Shades of Grey is considered, by some, the greatest romance novel they have ever read. I can't even live in a reality where a few people say it was an okay book.<br /><br />Goodbye cruel world.<br /><br />I never expected it to be this terrible. I never even thought it possible for a book this terrible to get published. But here it is, so we may as well warm up with a few quotes:<br /><br />\\"Anticipation runs bubbling like soda through my veins.\\"<br /><br />\\"My scalp prickles at the idea that maybe, just maybe, he might like me. After all, he did say he was glad Kate didn't do the interview. I hug myself with quiet glee, rocking from side to side, entertaining the possibility that he might like me for one brief moment.\\"<br /><br />\\"Grey smiles a dazzling, unguarded, natural, all-teeth-showing, glorious smile.\\"<br /><br />I think you get the point. Now let me delve a little further into why the writing made me want to vomit all over the inside of Charlie Tango:<br /><br />Extreme overuse of certain phrases: Oh my, I blush, I flush, holy crap, holy cow, crap, double crap, crapola, my breath hitches, his breath hitches, his eyes were hooded, and endless references to \\"down there.\\" Down where? Oh, down *there*. Cue the sound of a creaky old door opening and bats flying out. But seriously, one of these phrases shows up on every single page.  Also, in the middle of Ana using words like \\"crapola\\" and \\"jeez,\\" we suddenly see words that nobody ever heard of like \\"avuncular\\" and  \\"mercurial.\\"<br /><br />EL James has no idea that there's a difference between a subconscious and a conscience. Ana is constantly fighting with her \\"subconscious,\\" who is some sort of mix between a prudish librarian and a Ricki Lake audience member (\\"You a ho!\\"). She's that little voice that is always telling Ana what she's doing is wrong. You know, YOUR CONSCIENCE. You subconscious is just what it sounds like - below consciousness. You don't know it's there, and you definitely don't converse with it - unless maybe your name is Sybil.<br /><br />Now let's take a look at Anastasia Steele:<br /><br />1. She becomes mentally challenged the second Mister sculptured lips hangy-pants so much as hands her the remote control. At one point she actually asks, in all seriousness, if it's \\"legal to look that good?\\" She loses all ability to speak, think, walk, and use her medulla oblongata (don't look at me, Ana mentions it several times)at the mere sight of the man. Even Bella Swan had more wits about her when Edward was just hanging out eating a sandwich.<br /><br />I think that most of this relates to the fact that she has zero self-esteem. She thinks she's unattractive, klutzy, and worst of all - she's a brunette! Everyone knows brunettes are hideous!<br /><br />2. She doesn't care that Christian is controlling and abusive because he's just so darn hot. Her self-esteem is so low that she literally says she would do anything to be with him. \\"Anything\\" includes allowing herself to be whipped, spanked, and stalked across the country, even though he scares the life out of her. She says that she's in love with a man who she can't even make a joke around without being terrified that he's going to get angry and spank her.<br /><br />All of these things, and more, made Ana a weak, pathetic, joke of a character.<br /><br />Now let's take a look at Christian Grey:<br /><br />I don't care how hot he is, or how much money he has, the man has absolutely nothing to offer. Between his lack of personality, his lack of a sense of humor, and his general desire to beat women into submission as some sick form of \\"caring\\" for them, he is nothing but a disgusting self-centered pig. Oh he was abused as a child? He didn't get enough love? Fine, that's a perfectly reasonable explanation for him turning out the way he did, but it's no excuse for what he's doing to Ana. If two adults are both into that lifestyle and legitimately want to participate, then okay fine. Maybe that's how real BDSM relationships work, I don't know. What I do know is that's not the case with this book. Christian Grey is a manipulator, who takes a girl with no experience, no self-esteem, and no mind of her own, and convinces her to do things she's not comfortable with in order to \\"please him.\\" He wants to hurt her. He's stated it several times.<br /><br />And to top it all off, Ana is just giddy with joy over the idea that someone like him could actually like her. The idea that a man who is controlling, threatening, physically violent, and doesn't allow you the time or space to have your own life and thoughts, is actually romantic, is a seriously disturbing trend.<br /><br />And so I conclude, so I may get on with my life...<br /><br />As a self-published author myself, I am not sure I would want to make my millions off of a book like this. I'm not the type to sucker people out of their money and then laugh all the way to the bank. Oh I would still go to the bank, for sure. Most of us dream of being rich. But I would go to the bank with my head down. I would go to the bank with a promise in my mind to one day make up for perpetuating this twisted and dangerous version of love. I'd send a damn lot of my money to charity.<br /><br />That would almost be fifty shades of decent. But I won't hold my breath.hen okay fine. 1612130283|1.0|Dr. Oz devoted a show recently to \\"Fifty Shades of Grey,\\" which he called the \\"The Cure for Your Libido.\\"  Laura Berman, a gynecologist, and a psychologist all praised the novel as helpful for women bored about sex or sexless in their long-term relationships. I understood them to say that Fifty Shades helps such women have more sex and more rewarding sex with their partners, a laudable goal.  But why?  They said Fifty Shades provides desire fantasies for an intense focus on the sensations of sex because, in the novel, the woman was restrained physically and could only participate in partnered sex receptively.    BDSM is a topic about which I knew virtually nothing.  But it was easy to find research.  A large survey in Australia reported in 2008 [...] that, in the 2.2% of males and 1.3% of females who engaged in bondage and discipline sadomasochism, \\"they were no more likely to have been coerced into sexual activity, and were not significantly more likely to be unhappy or anxious-indeed, men who had engaged in BDSM scored significantly lower on a scale of psychological distress than other men. Engagement in BDSM was not significantly related to any sexual difficulties.\\"  Their \\"findings support the idea that BDSM is simply a sexual interest or subculture attractive to a minority, and for most participants not a pathological symptom of past abuse or difficulty with \\"normal\\" sex.\\"    On the other hand, the novel is about a college senior about 21 years old and a virgin to all sexual activity, even masturbation, who is about to graduate from a public west-coast university (a plausibility-ripper even for a bodice-ripper, but these novels are about fantasy, not reality).  She falls in love and lust with a preternaturally handsome and fabulously wealthy 27-year-old high-tech CEO, the eponymous Mr. Grey. Inside his wit, savoir faire, taste, and skills as a pianist, pilot, and sexual coach, he's a mess. He cannot accept any touch, let alone sexual.  He cannot discuss his feelings, especially about the cigarette burn scars all over his chest, about his crack-addict sex worker mother, or about the older married woman who took his virginity when he was 15 and conditioned him to need BDSM in order to be sexual.  But he is expert enough about how to treat women, after an admitted 15 relationships with submissives, that he induces hypersexuality in Steele by extreme application of conventional, safe, non-painful, even tender methods of inducing arousal and orgasm--\\"vanilla sex,\\" as he calls it.    Her soargasms and roargasms beget s'morgasms: she achieves progressively more intense orgasms, always upon his command, never without his approval, and, because his Big O immediately follows hers, gratifying her (see prior parenthetical phrase).  Then he rather systematically teaches her to associate increasing pain with sexual pleasure.  Though she comes to fear and hate this, she thought-bargains with herself that if she accepts the pain he doles out, she will gain leverage to make him tell his painful history, get over it, and give her the \\"more\\" that she wants: more true intimacy, more openness to accept her as an equal, and especially to accept her affectionate and sexual touch.  [you decide: is there any danger that this kind of thinking will put sex therapists out of business?]    Let's label this for what it is: codependency, the compulsive mode of egoic thought that seeks to manipulate other people into doing our will, loving us, and not abandoning us, instead of accepting that other people are what they are, that they are not subject to us, and that loving action, not manipulation, may (but may not) gain a return of love.    Nothing seems amiss in the novel's thesis that a woman can get sucked into being sexually abused by a man who provides high rewards and intermittent punishments.  Jim Pfaus, reviewing sexual conditioning, concluded that \\"it is each animal's unique experience with sexual behavior and sexual reward that molds the strength of responses made toward sexual incentives.\\"  [...]    And, isn't it possible that either or both of these factors (conditioning and codependency) may underpin why many women stay in abusive relationships--that there are rewards to be gotten despite the injuries?    But let's get back to the first question: why is Fifty Shades good for women?  Why are millions of women turned on by this book instead of turned off?  Student Ana Steele raises objections to Grey eventually and maintains some independence of will, even walks out on him in the end after an unmitigated beating, so is it as anti-feminist as I have portrayed?    Well, I don't think you can take what happens on the final page of a 514-page novel, one with an explicit sexual episode every thirty pages or so, as the lesson.  I think the lessons get inculcated throughout the book:  * hang in a relationship though he's mysterious because you'll eventually crack his mystery;  * hang in if the sex is great because it's more powerful than your willpower;  * hang in though he overwhelms you emotionally by alternating between brooding, anger, and indulgent humor because you fear his leaving will rob you of this emotional drug cocktail/roller-coaster;  * hang in because he is really motivated by love deep down rather than by his observable possessiveness and mental illness.    And hang in with reading Fifty Shades if you don't think we already have enough sexual, physical, and psychological abuse of women.  Hang in if you think overwhelming, romantic sex is better than an intimate relationship.  Hang in if you think women belong in the boudoir, not the boardroom. But I'm not going to buy the rest of the Grey trilogy.

While I really enjoyed 'Prey', and ate it up in the space of well inside a week, it never really soared like his best works have done.  As usual, the novel is based upon real science, upon real happenings in the world of science and technology that for me make his novels more compelling.  But the feeling of 'could this really happen?' somehow never hit me, as it did in Jurassic Park.  Yes, I know, it is a bit of a stretch to imagine bringing dinosaurs to life, but the extraordinary detail he delved into in JP, the highly detailed DNA methodologies that had the appearance of hard science at least made me really think.  In Prey, there were just too many 'Oh come on, that is ridiculous' types of developments that happened, things that went beyond any possible scientific explanation into sensationalism.  Also, the plotting just wasn't interesting enough.  No amazing twists, no 'wow' revelations, too much of a feeling that you knew in general where things were going.  Nonetheless, I give this novel a qualified recommendation.  If you are a Crichton fan and enjoyed novels like JP, Sphere, Disclosure, Airframe etc., you will like this one as well. 0393061310|4.0|From my blog:<br /><br />Title: Guns, Germs, and Steel<br />Author: Jared Diamond<br /> Hours (audiobook): ~5 abridged copy<br /> Summary: For anyone who has ever wondered why the heck the European and Asian civilizations were able to make ships and conquer other civilizations, this book tries to figure that out. A lot of the problems of today's world comes from the inequities of the civilizations of the past, and at first blush it can be confusing to students of history why the Spanish made ships and guns before the people of South America. Diamond explains that a lot of the development of history's civilizations depended on the domestic-able plants and animals in the area and the fact that Europe and Asia have a major axis that goes East to West where as Africa and the Americas are oriented North to South. This actually makes a big difference in terms of how easily crops can be adapted from neighbors. The major geographic barriers also play a role in how easily ideas and inventions can get spread between cultures. All of this paints a fairly convincing story of why people in one area progressed so much faster than others, giving us the world we currently have.<br /><br />As stated above, I only listened to the abridged audiobook of this book because that is what I had available, but I found the audiobook still very enlightening, unlike most abridged books that I've encountered. The full paper book is quite long, and so if you want an idea of the argument but not all the gory details, I do recommend the abridged version. In any case, I think this book makes a very important argument because it presents evidence that it wasn't something special about the genes of the people who became the conquerors, it was simply the luck of the land that they happened to inherit.<br /><br />More reviews at [...]

No tracks glared out as clear winners from the onset, bar one - \\"I'm addicted\\", probably Madonna's most powerful and hypnotically poetic dance track since 2005's \\"Jump\\". Alas, it was probably Madonna's worst ever lead single off any album - dreadful to say the least. Something that cannot go unnoticed on this album is the fact that Madonna seems to have borrowed several rifts from some of her former tracks. Girl Gone Wild starts off as a sister track to Confessions' \\"Sorry\\" and \\"I'm a Sinner\\" is a dead ringer for \\"Beautiful Stranger\\". On the whole, it's an interesting and hypnotic album with quite a few catchy numbers, but with several that are simply forgettable. This album is sound, but (and it pains me to say it) not quite the Madonna of days gone by. I probably echo the sentiments of many older fans when I ask that Madonna bring back the likes of Patrick Leonard and Shep Pettibone!

I don't find the violence overly disturbing or detailed (though if Martin could stop using the word \\"nipple\\" so much I'd be happier), and I found that I could still easily enjoy the deep characters and wonderfully woven plot for their own merit. 0312944926|3.0|Posting this review due to assignment in College English class.  If you have not read this book then don't read the below because it is a spoiler.  Although the reviews above give this book a 1 or 2 star rating, I believe it rates better.  I'm not a computer programmer so I could see how one maybe offended.  This is not Angels and Demons or The Davinci Code nor does it have the same mystical setting associated with those two.  This is just another book which makes you think.<br /><br />  The Plot and Structure of<br />  Dan Brown's Digital Fortress<br />  Robert Stout<br />  English 102<br />  Professor Weatherly<br />  June 13, 2012<br /> Digital Fortress has many twists and turns similar to any of the other books authored by Dan Brown.  Just when you believe you have captured the idea of the story he throws you into another intriguing turn.  As quoted by The Midwest Book Review, \\"Digital Fortress is the best and most realistic techno-thriller to reach the market in years... A chilling thrill a minute\\".<br /> Digital Fortress begins when the NSA experiences an issue with their top code breaking machine, TRANSLTR, \\"the single most expensive piece of computing equipment in the world- a machine the NSA swore did not exist\\".  TRANSLTR discovers a code that can not be broken and the NSA's best cryptographer, Susan Fletcher discovers a plot to bring down the machine and expose National intelligence secrets.<br /> Susan was preparing for a relaxing weekend with the man she loves when she received the call.  A weekend drastically changed into a life threatening and a time crucial dilemma.  Unknown to Susan at the time, her boyfriend, David Becker, received a call from Susan's boss, Trevor Strathmore, asking him to fly to Spain and retrieve a ring from a man, Ensei Tankado, who had passed away.<br /> When Susan arrives at work she learns that an ex-employee of the NSA, Tankado, has written an encrypted program which can not be broken and has an accomplice by the name of NDAKOTA.  While tracing an email sent between Tankado and NDAKOTA, Susan learns that a co-worker Greg Hale was Tankado's accomplice who was in the building with her.<br /> Mean while David arrives in Spain and learns that the ring he is looking for has been passed to a couple in the park where Tankado had died.  Every time David is getting close to achieving his task, he learns the ring was passed to another.  David spends his entire day tracking leads and eventually running for his life while still trying to locate the ring.<br /> Susan and Strathmore are pushed into making a decision, a decision between trusting that David will find the ring or shutdown TRANSLTR to stop the release of intelligence secrets.  Both believed that the ring contained the password needed to shutdown the code, but neither had 100 percent confidence that David would achieve his objective.  When they chose to reboot TRANSLTR, the cooling system is damaged and starts over heating, which explodes and takes the life of NSA's deputy director of operations.<br /> After the explosion of TRANSLTR, David narrowly escapes death and obtains the ring.  The head director of the NSA and all cryptographers are working to stop the code from being released and cripple the United States interests.  Susan and David are at a cross roads when they learn the rings letters do not stop the code from releasing.  Time is running out and with only seconds to spare, David and Susan found the kill code for the program saving a majority of the sensitive information.<br /> Relieved that they were successful and safe from harm Susan and David spend their weekend at Stone Manor.  They discuss David's future at the college and returning to teaching.  Unexpected to Susan, David proposes to her.<br /> I would recommend this book to anyone who loves to read about mystery and intrigue.  Like Publishers Weekly review, \\"In this fast-paced, plausible tale, Brown blurs the line between good and evil enough to delight patriots and paranoids alike\\".  I guarantee once you pick up this book, you will have trouble putting it down.<br /><br />  Reference:<br />Brown, D. (1998). Digital Fortress, St. Martin's Press, New York, NY

B005BX3NAA|4.0|It's been years since I paid attention to 80's hard rock in anything other than an ironic way. Those goofy white guys in their jean shorts with their guitar noodling and their over-the-top yelling, I thought. How could anyone take that stuff seriously anymore?<br /><br />It doesn't help that Axl Rose is known as an epic goofball with a hall-of-fame ego. He's not the type of guy it's easy to feel respect or admiration for. At least not when all you know about him is his disastrous 2002 MTV thing.<br /><br />All I needed was a few paragraphs from John Jeremiah Sullivan's essay \\"The Final Comeback of Axl Rose\\" and then I was instantly re-introduced to G N' R, and, for the first time, utterly enthralled. I've pasted those paragraphs below, and then added a correction at the end.<br /><br />---<br /><br />I. From the beginning, he's been the only indispensable white male rock dancer of his generation, the only one worth imitating in mockery. I consider the moment in the \\"Patience\\" video when he does slow-motion snaky slide-foot dance while letting his hands float down as if they were feathers in an airless room--one fleeting near-pause in their descent for each note that Slash emphasizes in his transition to the coda--the greatest white male rock dance moment of the video age. What Axl does is lovely, I'm sorry.<br /><br />II. I've read all this stuff that said Nirvana made Guns N' Roses obsolete. But Guns N' Roses were never made obsolete. They just sort of disintegrated. Closer to the case is that G N' R made Nirvana possible. When you think about the niche that Nirvana supposedly created and perfected--a megaband that indie snobs couldn't entirely disavow, no matter how badly they wanted to--G N' R got there first. They didn't get all the way there, I realize. They dressed silly. They didn't seem to know the difference between their good songs and their crap songs. But we have to remember, too, how they came along at a time when bands with singers who looked like Axl and thrust their hips unironically, and lead players who spread their legs and reeled off guitar-god noodling weren't supposed to be interesting, melodically or culturally or in any other way. G N' R were. They were also grotesque and crass and stupid sometimes, even most of the time. Even almost all of the time. But you always knew you were seeing something when you saw them.<br /><br />III. I'm left listening to \\"Patience\\" again. And I whistle along and wait for that voice, toward the end, when he goes, Ooooooo, I need you. OOOOOOO, I need you. And on the fist Ooooooo, he finds a note so tissue-shredding it conjures the image of someone peeling his own scalp back, like the skin of a grape. I have to be careful not to attempt to sing along with this part, because it makes me, like, sort of throw up a little bit. And on the second OOOOOOO, you picture just a naked glowing green skull that hangs there vibrating gape-mouthed in a hyperbaric chamber. Or whatever it is you picture.<br /><br />IV. And after each line he is gazing at the crowd with those strangely startled yet fearless eyes, as though we had just surprised him in his den, tearing into some carrion.<br /><br />V. Axl has said, \\"I sing in five or six different voices that are all part of me. It's not contrived.\\" I agree. One of them is an unexpectedly competent baritone. The most important of the voices, though, is Devil Woman. Devil Woman comes from a deeper part of Axl than do any of the other voices. Often she will not enter until nearer the end of a song. In fact, the dramatic conflict between Devil Woman and her sweet, melodic yang--the Axl who sings such lines as \\"Her hair reminds me of a warm, safe place\\" and \\"If you want to love me, then darling, don't refrain\\"--is precisely what resulted in Guns N' Roses' greatest songs. A lot of people will try to convince you that the band's street-tough image and attitude are what made them so massive. Of course, that's foolish. The \\"media\\" adore street-tough image and attitude and, via a curious circular maneuver, will frequently work to convince you that street-tough image and attitude are the reason you love some band, but you, the people of the world, tend to buy 15 million copies of a debut album because it's chockablock with hits. G N' R wrote four or five untouchable pop songs, and that's why we're still talking about them--that's why the mere fact of Axl's mounting a comeback was newsworthy.<br /><br />Take \\"Sweet Child o' Mine,\\" which, in my unassuming view, shares with the Wrens' \\"I've Made Enough Friends\\" the distinction of being the most perfectly achieved rock 'n' roll song of the past twenty years. It's not that you don't love it from the beginning, what with the killer riffs and the oddly antiquated-sounding chorus, yet a sword hangs over it. You think: This can't be everything. Come on--I mean, \\"Now and then when I see her face / It takes me away to that special place\\"? Then, around 5:04, she arrives. The song has veered minor-key by then, the clouds have begun to gather, and I never hear that awesome, intelligent solo that I don't imagine Axl's gone off somewhere at the start of it, to be by himself while his body undergoes certain changes. What I love is how when he comes back in, he comes in on top of himself (\\"five or six different voices that are all part of me\\"); he's not yet all the way finished with I, I, I, I, I, I, I, I when that fearsome timbre tears itself open. And what does she say, this Devil Woman? What does she always say, for that matter? Have you ever thought about it? I hadn't. \\"Sweet Child,\\" \\"Paradise City,\\" \\"November Rain,\\" \\"Patience,\\" they all come down to codas--Axl was a poet of the dark, unresolved coda--and to what do these codas themselves come down? Everybody needs somebody. Don't you think that you need someone? I need you. Oh, I need you. Where do we go? Where do we go now? Where do we go? I wanna go. Oh, won't you please take me home?<br /><br />---<br /><br />The thing that I think the essay gets wrong is painting G N' R as \\"crass and grotesque and stupid.\\" Try YouTubing some interviews with Axl. I think you'll be surprised by how articulate and thoughtful and even moral he is. True, he did some crass and grotesque and stupid things, but this clearly wasn't a guy who was just mindlessly spewing angry noise.age and attitude and, via a curious circular maneuver, will frequently work to convince you that street-tough image and attitude are the reason you love some band, but you, the people of the world, tend to buy 15 million copies of a debut album because it's chockablock with hits. G N' R wrote four or five untouchable pop songs, and that's why we're still talking about them--that's why the mere fact of Axl's mounting a comeback was newsworthy.<br /><br />Take \\"Sweet Child o' Mine,\\" which, in my unassuming view, shares with the Wrens' \\"I've Made Enough Friends\\" the distinction of being the most perfectly achieved rock 'n' roll song of the past twenty years. It's not that you don't love it from the beginning, what with the killer riffs and the oddly antiquated-sounding chorus, yet a sword hangs over it. You think: This can't be everything. Come on--I mean, \\"Now and then when I see her face / It takes me away to that special place\\"? Then, around 5:04, she arrives. The song has veered minor-key by then, the clouds have begun to gather, and I never hear that awesome, intelligent solo that I don't imagine Axl's gone off somewhere at the start of it, to be by himself while his body undergoes certain changes. What I love is how when he comes back in, he comes in on top of himself (\\"five or six different voices that are all part of me\\"); he's not yet all the way finished with I, I, I, I, I, I, I, I when that fearsome timbre tears itself open. And what does she say, this Devil Woman? What does she always say, for that matter? Have you ever thought about it? I hadn't. \\"Sweet Child,\\" \\"Paradise City,\\" \\"November Rain,\\" \\"Patience,\\" they all come down to codas--Axl was a poet of the dark, unresolved coda--and to what do these codas themselves come down? Everybody needs somebody. Don't you think that you need someone? I need you. Oh, I need you. Where do we go? Where do we go now? Where do we go? I wanna go. I think you'll be surprised by how articulate and thoughtful and even moral he is. True, he did some crass and grotesque and stupid things, but this clearly wasn't a guy who was just mindlessly spewing angry noise.

B000000OSG|4.0|It's been years since I paid attention to 80's hard rock in anything other than an ironic way. Those goofy white guys in their jean shorts with their guitar noodling and their over-the-top yelling, I thought. How could anyone take that stuff seriously anymore?<br /><br />It doesn't help that Axl Rose is known as an epic goofball with a hall-of-fame ego. He's not the type of guy it's easy to feel respect or admiration for. At least not when all you know about him is his disastrous 2002 MTV thing.<br /><br />All I needed was a few paragraphs from John Jeremiah Sullivan's essay \\"The Final Comeback of Axl Rose\\" and then I was instantly re-introduced to G N' R, and, for the first time, utterly enthralled. I've pasted those paragraphs below, and then added a correction at the end.<br /><br />---<br /><br />I. From the beginning, he's been the only indispensable white male rock dancer of his generation, the only one worth imitating in mockery. I consider the moment in the \\"Patience\\" video when he does slow-motion snaky slide-foot dance while letting his hands float down as if they were feathers in an airless room--one fleeting near-pause in their descent for each note that Slash emphasizes in his transition to the coda--the greatest white male rock dance moment of the video age. What Axl does is lovely, I'm sorry.<br /><br />II. I've read all this stuff that said Nirvana made Guns N' Roses obsolete. But Guns N' Roses were never made obsolete. They just sort of disintegrated. Closer to the case is that G N' R made Nirvana possible. When you think about the niche that Nirvana supposedly created and perfected--a megaband that indie snobs couldn't entirely disavow, no matter how badly they wanted to--G N' R got there first. They didn't get all the way there, I realize. They dressed silly. They didn't seem to know the difference between their good songs and their crap songs. But we have to remember, too, how they came along at a time when bands with singers who looked like Axl and thrust their hips unironically, and lead players who spread their legs and reeled off guitar-god noodling weren't supposed to be interesting, melodically or culturally or in any other way. G N' R were. They were also grotesque and crass and stupid sometimes, even most of the time. Even almost all of the time. But you always knew you were seeing something when you saw them.<br /><br />III. I'm left listening to \\"Patience\\" again. And I whistle along and wait for that voice, toward the end, when he goes, Ooooooo, I need you. OOOOOOO, I need you. And on the fist Ooooooo, he finds a note so tissue-shredding it conjures the image of someone peeling his own scalp back, like the skin of a grape. I have to be careful not to attempt to sing along with this part, because it makes me, like, sort of throw up a little bit. And on the second OOOOOOO, you picture just a naked glowing green skull that hangs there vibrating gape-mouthed in a hyperbaric chamber. Or whatever it is you picture.<br /><br />IV. And after each line he is gazing at the crowd with those strangely startled yet fearless eyes, as though we had just surprised him in his den, tearing into some carrion.<br /><br />V. Axl has said, \\"I sing in five or six different voices that are all part of me. It's not contrived.\\" I agree. One of them is an unexpectedly competent baritone. The most important of the voices, though, is Devil Woman. Devil Woman comes from a deeper part of Axl than do any of the other voices. Often she will not enter until nearer the end of a song. In fact, the dramatic conflict between Devil Woman and her sweet, melodic yang--the Axl who sings such lines as \\"Her hair reminds me of a warm, safe place\\" and \\"If you want to love me, then darling, don't refrain\\"--is precisely what resulted in Guns N' Roses' greatest songs. A lot of people will try to convince you that the band's street-tough image and attitude are what made them so massive. Of course, that's foolish. The \\"media\\" adore street-tough image and attitude and, via a curious circular maneuver, will frequently work to convince you that street-tough image and attitude are the reason you love some band, but you, the people of the world, tend to buy 15 million copies of a debut album because it's chockablock with hits. G N' R wrote four or five untouchable pop songs, and that's why we're still talking about them--that's why the mere fact of Axl's mounting a comeback was newsworthy.<br /><br />Take \\"Sweet Child o' Mine,\\" which, in my unassuming view, shares with the Wrens' \\"I've Made Enough Friends\\" the distinction of being the most perfectly achieved rock 'n' roll song of the past twenty years. It's not that you don't love it from the beginning, what with the killer riffs and the oddly antiquated-sounding chorus, yet a sword hangs over it. You think: This can't be everything. Come on--I mean, \\"Now and then when I see her face / It takes me away to that special place\\"? Then, around 5:04, she arrives. The song has veered minor-key by then, the clouds have begun to gather, and I never hear that awesome, intelligent solo that I don't imagine Axl's gone off somewhere at the start of it, to be by himself while his body undergoes certain changes. What I love is how when he comes back in, he comes in on top of himself (\\"five or six different voices that are all part of me\\"); he's not yet all the way finished with I, I, I, I, I, I, I, I when that fearsome timbre tears itself open. And what does she say, this Devil Woman? What does she always say, for that matter? Have you ever thought about it? I hadn't. \\"Sweet Child,\\" \\"Paradise City,\\" \\"November Rain,\\" \\"Patience,\\" they all come down to codas--Axl was a poet of the dark, unresolved coda--and to what do these codas themselves come down? Everybody needs somebody. Don't you think that you need someone? I need you. Oh, I need you. Where do we go? Where do we go now? Where do we go? I wanna go. Oh, won't you please take me home?<br /><br />---<br /><br />The thing that I think the essay gets wrong is painting G N' R as \\"crass and grotesque and stupid.\\" Try YouTubing some interviews with Axl. I think you'll be surprised by how articulate and thoughtful and even moral he is. True, he did some crass and grotesque and stupid things, but this clearly wasn't a guy who was just mindlessly spewing angry noise.age and attitude and, via a curious circular maneuver, will frequently work to convince you that street-tough image and attitude are the reason you love some band, but you, the people of the world, tend to buy 15 million copies of a debut album because it's chockablock with hits. I have to be careful not to attempt to sing along with this part, because it makes me, like, sort of throw up a little bit. And on the second OOOOOOO, you picture just a naked glowing green skull that hangs there vibrating gape-mouthed in a hyperbaric chamber. Or whatever it is you picture.<br /><br />IV.  And after each line he is gazing at the crowd with those strangely startled yet fearless eyes, as though we had just surprised him in his den, tearing into some carrion.<br /><br />V.  Axl has said, \\"I sing in five or six different voices that are all part of me. It's not contrived.\\" I agree. One of them is an unexpectedly competent baritone. The most important of the voices, though, is Devil Woman. Devil Woman comes from a deeper part of Axl than do any of the other voices. Often she will not enter until nearer the end of a song. In fact, the dramatic conflict between Devil Woman and her sweet, melodic yang--the Axl who sings such lines as \\"Her hair reminds me of a warm, safe place\\" and \\"If you want to love me, then darling, don't refrain\\"--is precisely what resulted in Guns N' Roses' greatest songs. A lot of people will try to convince you that the band's street-tough image and attitude are what made them so massive. Of course, that's foolish. The \\"media\\" adore street-tough image and attitude and, via a curious circular maneuver, will frequently work to convince you that street-tough image and attitude are the reason you love some band, but you, the people of the world, tend to buy 15 million copies of a debut album because it's chockablock with hits. G N' R wrote four or five untouchable pop songs, and that's why we're still talking about them--that's why the mere fact of Axl's mounting a comeback was newsworthy.<br /><br />Take \\"Sweet Child o' Mine,\\" which, in my unassuming view, shares with the Wrens' \\"I've Made Enough Friends\\" the distinction of being the most perfectly achieved rock 'n' roll song of the past twenty years. It's not that you don't love it from the beginning, what with the killer riffs and the oddly antiquated-sounding chorus, yet a sword hangs over it. You think: This can't be everything. Come on--I mean, \\"Now and then when I see her face / It takes me away to that special place\\"? Then, around 5:04, she arrives. The song has veered minor-key by then, the clouds have begun to gather, and I never hear that awesome, intelligent solo that I don't imagine Axl's gone off somewhere at the start of it, to be by himself while his body undergoes certain changes. What I love is how when he comes back in, he comes in on top of himself (\\"five or six different voices that are all part of me\\"); he's not yet all the way finished with I, I, I, I, I, I, I, I when that fearsome timbre tears itself open. And what does she say, this Devil Woman? What does she always say, for that matter? Have you ever thought about it? I hadn't. \\"Sweet Child,\\" \\"Paradise City,\\" \\"November Rain,\\" \\"Patience,\\" they all come down to codas--Axl was a poet of the dark, unresolved coda--and to what do these codas themselves come down? Everybody needs somebody. Don't you think that you need someone? I need you. Oh, I need you. Where do we go? Where do we go now? Where do we go? I wanna go. Oh, won't you please take me home?<br /><br />---<br /><br />The thing that I think the essay gets wrong is painting G N' R as \\"crass and grotesque and stupid.\\" Try YouTubing some interviews with Axl. I think you'll be surprised by how articulate and thoughtful and even moral he is. True, he did some crass and grotesque and stupid things, but this clearly wasn't a guy who was just mindlessly spewing angry noise.gh image and attitude and, via a curious circular maneuver, will frequently work to convince you that street-tough image and attitude are the reason you love some band, but you, the people of the world, tend to buy 15 million copies of a debut album because it's chockablock with hits. 0307352145|2.0|The book offers some great insights and research for some of its claims, but the research was often overly simplified or one-sided and drew overarching conclusions. One example is of her discussion of the amygdala and how it is the basically the core of your \\"flight or fight\\" response to dangerous or uncomfortable stimuli. Not once does she even mention the words sympathetic nervous system which in fact is the network of nerves that regulates your flight or fight response. She writes something like 'when a frisbee is headed toward you or snake is hissing at you it's your amygdala which tells you to run.' Not true! Yes, it is purported that the amygdala is the emotional center of your brain, but your amygdala is not in any way involved in these reflex responses. These stimuli do not go through your amygdala but pass to your sympathetic nervous system which terminates in the adrenal glands which release adrenaline. However, if you do have an emotional (not reflex) response to a certain stimuli that makes you nervous or uncomfortable it may pass through your amygdala and onto your sympathetic nervous system which then releases adrenaline and makes you even more uncomfortable. This criticism to some may seem as though it's missing the point, but to me it shows that she makes claims based on research that she can't even correctly explain herself. There is a vast difference between an emotional response to a stimuli and reflex to a object moving toward your head.<br /><br />Another example when she talks about how groups may sap the creativity of individuals, she focuses entirely on only that side. At the end of the chapter she offers a couple of paragraphs on how groups may actually be beneficial in her cafe example, but she fails to give due credit to the fact that being in groups engenders the exchange of ideas and may sprout innovation.<br /><br />In the chapter the temperament destiny she almost completely bases all of her conclusions on Kagan's study and presents itas gospel as if there are no competing theories. In her author's description its says she went to Princeton and Harvard Law, but it is pretty clear that she is not an academic and oversimplifies research and draws heavy conclusions. With that being said, the book exposed me to new ideas and piqued my interest.

0307716546|3.0|The premise of \\"Afterwards\\" by Rosamund Lupton is similar to that of \\"The Lovely Bones\\":  a dead  (or nearly dead) person communicates with others in an attempt to uncover a crime.  In the case of \\"Afterwards,\\" the crime is arson, and the beginning of the book is well done--fast-paced, interesting, and  engaging.  The main character rushes into a burning school to save her teenaged daughter.  Both mother and daughter are severely injured in the fire, and their spirits communicate with one another as they  lie in comas in the local hospital.  Then minor characters (father, son) become involved as Mom tries to discover who set the fire and why.<br /><br />After this superb beginning, this novel simply bogs down.  It is too long in the middle, and there is far too much psychological analysis for my taste. Of course the reader wants to know underlying complexity of motivation, but too much is too much.  Mother and daughter communicate, and parts of this section are confusing.  It is occasionally difficult to know who is speaking.  Mom finds out things she never knew about her family--surprise, surprise--and the incredible and undying love of a mother for her child is repeatedly reinforced.<br /><br />In the end, the reader discovers who set the fire, and the answer is very unexpected. There are any number of likely suspects, and the motivations of each are explored, but most readers will not guess the identity of the arsonist.<br /><br />To sum up, this was a good story, but not great.  I notice that many other reviewers have mentioned Lupton's other book, \\"Sister.\\"  I plan to read this book to see if I can get a better handle on her storytelling.  For now, count me in the ambivalent category.

B0000247D1|3.0|Listening to Bruckner could be likened to lying on your back and looking up at the constellations as they revolve around the axis of the earth. It may not be as viscerally exciting, say, as a visit to the House of the Rising Sun in downtown Bangkok but there is nothing to say that it should be boring. To that end, there is no substitute for imaginative phrasing in this domain.<br /><br />No one doubts that Gunter Wand is a competent Brucknerian. When allied with the Berlin Philharmonic and an atmospheric recording, the output is always going to be listenable in the least - to wit, this performance of the Fourth Symphony (cobbled-together though it is from various performances). At times, the array rises to the occasion: the `Holy of Holies' chorale at the heart of the first movement and the coda of the finale are virtuosity incarnate. And yet so often, to my ears at least, the affair is militated by prosaic phrasing (just listen to the opening three minutes or so of the first movement).<br /><br />But there is a deeper failure here: this symphony is Bruckner's most dangerous excursion into \\"the forests of the night.\\" This performance, sad to say, but fitfully addresses the age-old dynamic of man versus nature. What the hell: it is time for a gratuitous quote to illustrate this point (and all the more so as it is more inspired than anything from yours truly). It was written at the dawn of civilisation:<br /><br /> . . . . . It was then that the lord Gilgamesh turned his thoughts to the Country of the Living; on the Land of Cedars the lord Gilgamesh reflected. He said to his servant Enkidu: \\"I have not established my name stamped on bricks as my destiny decreed; therefore I will go to the country where the cedar is felled. I will set up my name in the place where the names of famous men are written, and where no man's name is written yet I will raise a monument to the gods. Because of the evil that is in the land, we will journey into the great forest and destroy the evil; for in the forest lives Humbaba whose name is `Hugeness', a ferocious giant.\\"<br /><br />But Enkidu sighed bitterly and said, \\"When I lived with the wild beasts in the wilderness I discovered the forest; its length is ten thousand leagues in every direction. Enlil has appointed Humbaba to guard it and armed him with sevenfold terrors; terrible to all flesh is Humbaba. When he roars it is like the torrent of the storm, his breath is like fire and his jaws are death itself. He guards the cedars so well that when a wild heifer stirs in the forest, though she is sixty leagues distant, he hears her. What man would willingly walk into that country and explore its depths? I tell you, weakness overpowers whoever goes near it: it is not an equal struggle when one fights with Humbaba;. he is a great warrior, a battering-ram. Gilgamesh, the Watchman of the Forest never sleeps.\\"<br /><br />Gilgamesh replied: \\"Where is the man who can clamber to Heaven? Only the gods live forever with glorious Shamash, but as for us men, our days are numbered, our occupations are a breath of wind. How is it that you're afraid already! I will go first although I am your lord . . . Then if I fall I leave behind me a name that is everlasting; men will say of me, `Gilgamesh has fallen in battle with the ferocious Humbaba.'\\" . . . . .<br /><br />This dimension is near-absent in this performance of the Fourth. It does not bespeak danger; the immensity of Nature and its indifference to our aspirations are likewise underplayed. It's a Bruckner Fourth that is commodious for an age that would rather forget the primordial roots of Homo Sapiens.<br /><br />So by all means lap up the luxuriant orchestral response. With effort, convince yourself that the Kapellmeister on the podium - and a talented one at that - is  a great conductor. And switch on the fluorescent lights as you listen on: they're a cheap and efficient way to dispel the darkness.<br /><br />If, on the other hand you want to venture withGilgamesh into the great forest, press either of these into service:<br /><br />[[ASIN:B0009NDKUU Bruckner: Symphony No. 4]]<br /><br />[[ASIN:B00000IG30 Bruckner: Symphony No. 4]]Gilgamesh into the great forest, press either of these into service:<br /><br />[[ASIN:B0009NDKUU Bruckner: Symphony No. 4]]<br /><br />[[ASIN:B00000IG30 Bruckner: Symphony No. 4]]

B00005JM8V|5.0|It took me over a month to watch Shoah in its 9 1/2 -hour entirety. Unrelentingly intense, each moment is profound.  This documentary does not contain any archival footage, graphic photos, or reenactments.  And yet, it is the most important and powerful documentary about the Holocaust ever made.  Shoah is comprised entirely of the eyewitness testimony of Holocaust survivors, non-Jewish bystanders, and German perpetrators.  Film of the interviewees is paired with emotionally devastating present-day images of the death camps and the roads, countryside and railroads that led to them.<br /><br />Centered around the Nazi extermination camps of Chelmno, Treblinka, Auschwitz, and the Warsaw Ghetto, Claude Lanzmann, himself a Jewish survivor of the Holocaust, is extremely skilled at interviewing all of the Holocaust witnesses, regardless of the role they played.  No explanation or opinion of the interviews are given, and none are needed.  You can see for yourself the anguish of the survivors, the deep-seated antisemitism of the bystanders, and the detached, complex testimony of the perpetrators.<br /><br />Watching Shoah will lend a powerful feeling of intimacy and immediacy to the Holocaust that will help the viewer begin to understand just why it's such an important subject to learn about and remember.  Historically significant and emotionally powerful, Shoah should be required viewing for every person, everywhere.
